Simon v. New York


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, New York County


22 Misc. 2d 718; 197 N.Y.S.2d 288; 1960 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3860



January 5, 1960 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Plaintiff's motion is denied; defendants' motion is granted. Settle order.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff sought a judgment declaring that 1959 N.Y. Laws 369 and New York City, N.Y., Local Law No. 47 (1959) were unconstitutional and permanently restraining defendants, city and associated entities, from imposing and collecting the taxicab tax. Defendants filed a motion to dismiss the complaint.


OVERVIEW: 1959 N.Y. Laws 369 provided for a tax of up to 10 cents per trip, fare, or use of a taxicab, to be paid by the passenger. Plaintiff argued that the tax was levied on intangible personal property of the hirer in violation of N.Y. Const. art. XVI, § 3, because no orderly person could be refused conveyance at his request unless the taxicab was previously engaged, pursuant to New York City, N.Y., Admin. Code § 436-2.0(b)(24)(b), and, thus, the passenger had a contract of value and intangible personal property involving his personal rights and relationship with the operator. The court held that N.Y. Const. art. XVI, § 3 was intended to avoid taxation of large quantities of saleable assets of substantial monetary value simply because of their presence and possession within the state and that when such intangible property was put to use in the flow of trade and commerce, excise tax became imposable. The court further held that a person's right to use a taxicab had no monetary value because there was nothing to sell and that it was taxable when availed of. The tax was not an ad valorem tax because it was invariable and fixed before determination of the ultimate total charge.


OUTCOME: The court denied plaintiff's motion for a finding that the tax on taxicab fares and use was unconstitutional and an injunction on defendants' imposition of the tax, and granted defendants' motion to dismiss the complaint.


HEADNOTES

Taxation -- tax on hiring of taxicab -- tax constitutional (Local Laws, 1959, No. 47 of City of New York; L. 1959, ch. 369, § 5; cf. N. Y. Const., art. XVI, § 3).

New York City's tax of 10 cents on each hiring of a taxicab (Local Laws, 1959, No. 47 of City of New York; L. 1959, ch. 369, § 5) was valid and constitutional. It did not violate the State Constitution (art. XVI, § 3) or the Federal Constitution (14th Amdt.).

 

2500 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Meola


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


County Court of New York, Orange County


19 Misc. 2d 837; 194 N.Y.S.2d 823; 1959 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2324



December 24, 1959 



DISPOSITION: The conviction is reversed on the law and the fine is remitted.

Submit order.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment of a Court of Special Sessions of the Town of Newburgh, Orange County (New York) convicting and fining her for failing to dim her headlights in violation of N.Y. Veh. & Traf. Law § 15(3).


OVERVIEW: Defendant claimed that N.Y. Veh. & Traf. Law § 15(3) was unconstitutional. The court reversed the conviction and remitted the fine. The court stated that the language of the statute governing each situation to which it applied was so indefinite, unclear, and ambiguous as not to give "unequivocal warning" to citizens of the rule which was to be obeyed and that application of the statute in each of the alternative situations was therefore unconstitutional. The court observed that the law-abiding motorist on the highway at night had many rules to carry in mind while operating his motor vehicle and that to require him, in addition, to gauge the quality of the lighting and the quantity of the traffic, and to determine whether constantly changing lighting or the erratic flow of traffic rendered his high headlight beam "unnecessary" or "impracticable," would result in a heavy burden indeed. The court stated that the motorist would look in vain to § 15(3) for any unequivocal warning of the rules which he was expected to obey. The court determined that to require him to make such a determination accurately, without such warning, or suffer a penalty, was not due process of law.


OUTCOME: The court reversed on the law the judgment of the trial court convicting defendant of failing to dim her headlights and remitted the fine.


HEADNOTES

Motor vehicles -- failure to dim lights -- section 15 (subd. 3) of Vehicle and Traffic Law, providing that operator of motor vehicle approaching motor vehicle in opposite direction lower headlights, indefinite, unclear and ambiguous, and unconstitutional -- defendant's conviction reversed and fine remitted.

Subdivision 3 of section 15 of the Vehicle and Traffic Law provides as follows: "Provided that, whenever a vehicle approaching from ahead is within five hundred feet * * * the headlamps, if of the multiple beam type, shall be operated so that dazzling light does not interfere with the driver of the approaching vehicle * * * and, whenever the highway is so lighted or traffic thereon is such that illumination of the highway for more than two hundred feet ahead of the vehicle by lights on such vehicle is unnecessary or [***2] impracticable, the head lamps, if of the multiple beam type, shall be operated with the lowermost distribution of light in use." The language of the statute is indefinite, unclear, and ambiguous, and is unconstitutional. Defendant's conviction of violating the statute is reversed and the fine is remitted.

 

2501 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Burns


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


County Court of New York, Niagara County


23 Misc. 2d 636; 207 N.Y.S.2d 762; 1959 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2397



December 15, 1959 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant demurred to an indictment on the charge of unauthorized conduct of bingo games in violation of N.Y. Gen. Mun. Law § 14-G.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was indicted for unlawfully operating bingo games in the city. The District Attorney in his answering brief made the concession that N.Y. Gen. Mun. Law § 496 was confusing with regard to the application of the entire Bingo Licensing Law to a community where there had not been a successful approving referendum vote. However, the District Attorney stated that legislative intent was the controlling principle in interpreting any statute. The court found that none of the cases submitted by the District Attorney expressly stated that the playing of bingo or conducting of bingo games, especially free or entertainment bingo, was legal only where played in a community that had approved it by referendum. Moreover, in those communities where there has been no such referendum or where a referendum has been held but defeated, the people of that community were entitled to play free or entertainment bingo. Because the legislature did not state in clear, understandable, and unequivocal language that its intent was that no bingo be played in the State except under the State's control, defendant could not be charged with a violation of § 14-G.


OUTCOME: The court allowed defendant's demurrer and dismissed the indictment. Defendant's bail was to be exonerated.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Crimes -- bingo -- free bingo no crime (N. Y. Const., art. I, § 9, subd. 2; General Municipal Law, art. 14-G).

Conducting a bingo game, solely as entertainment and free of charge therefor, is not a crime, even in a municipality which has by referendum disapproved of bingo. (N. Y. Const., art. I, § 9, subd. 2; General Municipal Law, art. 14-G; Executive Law, art. 19-B.)

 

2502 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


London v. Wagner


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special and Trial Term, New York County


22 Misc. 2d 360; 195 N.Y.S.2d 550; 1959 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2422



December 11, 1959 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner landowner filed an action against respondent city and alleged that New York City, N.Y., Administrative Code § J41-2.4 (k) was unconstitutional because it granted a tax exemption to landlords whose properties were tax exempt and not to landlords who properties were decontrolled.


OVERVIEW: The landowner owned a building that was a substandard dwelling. The landowner then altered and repaired the building. Subsequently the landowner applied to the city for a tax exemption. The city refused because the landowner's property was decontrolled and argued that the tax exemption only applied to rent-controlled buildings pursuant to § J41-2.4(k). The landowner then filed an action against the city and alleged that the provision was unconstitutional. The court found that the law was valid. Furthermore, the court found that the law was constitutional even though it distinguished between rent controlled and decontrolled buildings. The court reasoned that the law was not discriminatory because statutory distinctions were permissible and there was reasonable grounds to support them. The court also found that prior to the law landlords who were subject to rent control had little inducement to improve their buildings because they could not get a return for their investment. Therefore, there was no public purpose in extending the inducement to decontrolled properties. The court found in favor of the city.


OUTCOME: The court found in favor of the city in the landlord's action challenging the constitutionality of a city ordinance.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Municipal corporations -- tax abatement for improvements in substandard multiple dwellings -- local law amendments withdrawing such tax abatements from rent-decontrolled dwellings, constitutional.

1. Pursuant to legislative authority (N. Y. Const., art. IX, § 9; Tax Law, § 5-h), the City of New York enacted a local law, effective in 1956 (Administrative Code of City of New York, § J41-2.4), authorizing the granting of 12 years' tax abatement to owners of substandard multiple dwellings who made substantial improvements therein. It subsequently developed that landlords of rent-decontrolled multiple dwellings (Residential Rent Law, L. 1946, ch. 274, as amd.), as distinguished from controlled ones, did not need such inducement. Therefore, the Legislature amended section 5-h of the Tax Law so as to withdraw such tax abatement after July 1, 1958 from rent-decontrolled dwellings (L. 1958, ch. 901, § 1) and so as to authorize municipalities, until June 1, 1959, "to adopt and amend local laws" accordingly (L. 1958, ch. 901, § 2). The City of New York adopted an amendment accordingly on January 19, 1959 (Local Laws, 1959, No. 14 of City of New York [Administrative Code of City [***2] of New York, § J41-2.4, subd. k]). However, the amendment had not been on the city councilmen's desks for at least seven days (New York City Charter, § 37; General Construction Law, § 110). The city validly and constitutionally cured this irregularity on September 22, 1959 (after June 1, 1959) by properly enacting a local law which gave full force and effect to the earlier local law as of its original date (Local Laws, 1959, No. 408 of City of New York).

2. Petitioners, owners of substandard but rent-decontrolled multiple dwellings, made substantial improvements therein, and the city's department of buildings certified on January 7, 1959 that the work complied with the local law (Administrative Code of City of New York, § J41-2.4) and was entitled to an annual tax exemption on the sum of $ 25,222. But when petitioners applied for the tax abatement on May 6, 1959, the city's tax commission validly refused, because of the amendment which the city had irregularly enacted on January 19, 1959 (§ J41-2.4, subd. k) and because of the curative Local Law No. 408 which the city validly enacted thereafter on September 22, 1959. Accordingly, petitioners' proceedings to review the denial [***3] of tax abatements (Civ. Prac. Act, art. 78) are dismissed, after trial.

3. There was no contract between the city and petitioners, nor were petitioners denied the equal protection of the laws as to any vested right.

4. Petitioners asked the Temporary State Housing Rent Commission to place their buildings under rent control again, and the commission refused; but this is immaterial to the issue of constitutionality of the local law amendments denying tax-abatement to rent-decontrolled dwellings.

 

2503 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Feinberg


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of General Sessions of County of New York


19 Misc. 2d 433; 193 N.Y.S.2d 937; 1959 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2439



December 10, 1959 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Motion denied. Submit order.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant, who was accused in a 21 count indictment of perjury and contempt, filed a motion to dismiss the indictment on the ground that the grand jury violated his rights under N.Y. Const. art. I, § 6 and the Fourteenth Amendment to the U.S. Constitution when it subpoenaed him and administered the oath to him.


OVERVIEW: In response to a subpoena, defendant testified before the grand jury on 12 separate occasions. He was sworn on November 13, 1958, and before any questions were put to him, he was advised that the grand jury was investigating whether there was a conspiracy of a criminal nature to commit the crimes of bribery and taking of unlawful fees. The Assistant District Attorney thereupon warned him that he could be a defendant in criminal proceedings resulting from the investigation. The crimes of perjury and contempt with which defendant was charged in the indictment arose from testimony given by him before the grand jury and were alleged to have been committed on dates subsequent to the grant of immunity on November 13, 1958. The court found that under N.Y. Penal Law § 2447, defendant was not entitled to remain silent because he obtained full and complete immunity from prosecution. However, under § 2447, despite the grant of immunity, he could be prosecuted for any perjury or contempt committed in answering, failing to answer, or in producing or failing to produce evidence. Defendant was examined merely as a witness whose testimony was sought to be used against others.


OUTCOME: The court denied defendant's motion.


HEADNOTES

Witnesses -- immunity -- even though witness might have been prospective defendant, if he is given complete immunity from prosecution as to matters on which he testifies he can be indicted for perjury or contempt in his answers (Penal Law, §§ 2447, 584; N. Y. Const., art. I, § 6).

1. When a Grand Jury or other "competent authority" gives a witness complete immunity from prosecution "for or on account of any * * * matter * * * concerning which" he shall give answer or produce evidence, "he may nevertheless be prosecuted * * * for any perjury or contempt committed in answering, or failing to answer, or in producing or failing to produce evidence" (Penal Law, §§ 2447, 584, as amd. by L. 1953, ch. 891). Such a complete grant of immunity is fully coextensive with his constitutional privilege against self incrimination (N. Y. Const., art. I, § 6); hence it is immaterial that, otherwise, the witness might have been a prospective defendant in the investigation.

2. Accordingly, his motion to dismiss a subsequent indictment against him for perjury and contempt in his immune testimony is denied.

3. The United States Constitution (14th Amdt.) has [***2] no application. The privilege against self incrimination is not one of "the privileges or immunities of citizens of the United States".

4. Although a defendant must go through a trial before he can appeal from an order such as this one denying his motion to dismiss the indictment, whereas the People now have a statutory right of appeal from such an order of dismissal of the indictment, these considerations are for the Legislature, not the courts.

Dankner v. New York


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, New York County


20 Misc. 2d 557; 194 N.Y.S.2d 975; 1959 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2448



December 9, 1959 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The defendants' motion to dismiss the complaint and granting judgment in favor of the defendants is granted in all respects.

Settle order.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs sought a judgment declaring unconstitutional New York City, N.Y., Admin. Code § D26-3.10 (1957) (New York City, N.Y., Local Laws No. 79 (1957)) and to restrain defendants, a city and others, from proceeding to enforce these provisions. Defendants filed a motion for judgment on the pleadings, dismissing the complaint on the ground of legal insufficiency and declaring the legislation constitutional.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs argued that the legislation was unconstitutional insofar as it required installation of central heating and hot-water equipment in tenement properties containing more than 10 apartments and contended that the enactment acted to deprive the owners of property without due process, that it did not actually promote the public health and safety of the tenants, and that it was in conflict with the N.Y. Mult. Dwell. Law. The court found that the city council held hearings prior to the enactment of the sections in question and made findings which sufficiently set forth the legislative purpose. The court felt that this was a reasonable use of police power because there was a close relationship between this use of the power and the evil sought to be remedied and there was no requirement of unreasonable expenditure when considered in the light of the objective to be accomplished. The court also held that the provisions in the enactment under attack were not inconsistent with N.Y. Mult. Dwell. Law § 3(4) and were fully justified under the powers vested in the local legislative body.


OUTCOME: The court granted defendants' motion and entered judgment for defendants.


HEADNOTES

Municipal corporations -- regulation of tenement properties -- section D26-3.10 of Administrative Code of City of New York, requiring installation of central heating and hot-water equipment in tenement properties containing more than 10 apartments, not unconstitutional -- statute not inconsistent with Multiple Dwelling Law ( § 3, subd. 4).

1. Section D26-3.10 of the Administrative Code of the City of New York (Local Laws, 1957, No. 79 of City of New York), requiring installation of central heating and hot-water equipment in tenement properties containing more than 10 apartments, is not unconstitutional. It was a reasonable exercise of police power.

2. The enactment is not inconsistent with subdivision 4 of section 3 of the Multiple Dwelling Law.

Quick v. Owego


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Broome County


25 Misc. 2d 697; 211 N.Y.S.2d 806; 1959 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2491



December 4, 1959 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff property owners moved for summary judgment in their declaratory judgment action against defendant town. The property owners sought to have a zoning ordinance declared void for the failure of the town to comply with the requirements of N.Y. Town Law § 264 in its enactment.


OVERVIEW: The property owners sought to convert their property from residential to commercial uses. They were prohibited from so doing because of the provisions of the challenged ordinance. The property owners pointed out that the town did not include the map of the zoned areas in its town board minutes, as required by N.Y. Town Law § 264. Although the town alleged that the map was freely available, there was no incorporation of it by reference in the ordinance and the map was not attached or included. The court granted the property owners their requested relief, holding the ordinance invalid. The State law was to be strictly enforced in all cases. The use districts were not described in the text of the ordinance. Consequently, a reference to the place of filing of the zoning map which purported to describe those districts was a vital requisite.


OUTCOME: The town's zoning ordinance was declared void. The court granted the property owners' motion for summary judgment.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Municipal corporations -- zoning -- town zone map must be "entered in the minutes of the town board" (Town Law, § 264).

Special Term declared invalid a town zoning ordinance whose very large map of the zones was not "entered in the minutes of the town board" (Town Law, § 264) but was displayed on a wall or easel in the town clerk's office.

 

2506 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Schildhaus v. Gilroy


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, New York County


22 Misc. 2d 524; 195 N.Y.S.2d 124; 1959 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2503



December 3, 1959 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Motion denied and judgment on pleadings granted to defendant. Settle order.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff lessor moved for judgment on the pleadings against defendant city in an action seeking a declaratory judgment that New York City, N.Y., Admin. Code § D 26-3.1 was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: The lessor alleged that regulation of multiple dwellings was a state affair and that the State of New York had pre-empted the field when it enacted the N.Y. Multiple Dwelling Law. The lessor alleged that the local ordinance was inconsistent with the state law. The local ordinance (1) required owners to pay a registration fee, whereas the N.Y. Multiple Dwelling Law did not provide for the payment of a fee; (2) required corporations owning multiple dwellings to file the names and addresses of their officers; and (3) fixed heavier penalties for failure to register than those prescribed in the N.Y. Multiple Dwelling Law. The court upheld the local ordinance, awarding judgment to the city. The court found that the fee requirement was not inconsistent with state law. The name and address requirement had been added to state law in 1959. Finally, the heavier penalties should be determined in criminal court where the lessor was already being prosecuted for his second violation. The court found that a declaratory judgment action was an improper avenue to obtain a collateral attack on a criminal judgment.


OUTCOME: The court denied the lessor's motion for judgement. The court granted judgment on pleadings to the city.


HEADNOTES

Declaratory judgments -- judgment on pleadings -- plaintiff's motion for judgment on pleadings in action for judgment declaring "Multiple Dwelling Code, particularly Section D 26-3.1" unconstitutional, denied -- defendant granted judgment on pleadings.

1. In this action for a judgment declaring that the "Multiple Dwelling Code, particularly Section D 26-3.1" is unconstitutional, null and void, in that the City of New York had no authority to enact measures relating to multiple dwellings which are inconsistent with the State's Multiple Dwelling Law, plaintiff's motion for judgment on the pleadings is denied. A local law is not inconsistent with a State statute merely because it provides for higher penalties, or requires a registration fee while the State statute does not. Moreover, sections 3 and 365 of the Multiple Dwelling Law authorize a city to adopt and enforce more restrictive measures than those provided in the State law.

2. Plaintiff, who has been convicted of a second violation of the local law, also attacks the validity of a provision permitting a fine up to $ 1,000 and imprisonment [***2] up to one year in the event of a second conviction. As the purpose of a declaratory judgment is to obtain a guide for future action, ordinarily an action for such a judgment will not be entertained where there is an adequate legal remedy. The remedies of this plaintiff were or are adequate in the criminal court or appellate tribunal.

3. Judgment on the pleadings is granted to defendant.

 

2507 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Piliero v. Allstate Ins. Co.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Queens County


22 Misc. 2d 415; 195 N.Y.S.2d 89; 1959 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2538



November 27, 1959 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff driver brought a declaratory judgment action against defendant insurer in an attempt to establish the insurer's obligation to defend the driver regarding an accident he had while driving the car of the insured.


OVERVIEW: The driver parked cars in the parking lot of a restaurant for tips. The restaurant did not pay the driver for the service but he did have the restaurant's permission. The driver was involved in an accident with the car of the insured. The insurer refused to defend the driver in an action against him based on the accident. The driver brought a declaratory action against the insurer. The court found in favor of the driver, holding that he was entitled to the same protection and benefits that the policy conferred upon the named insured. The court found that the parking lot was not a public lot and that when he drove the insured's car, at the latter's request, became an insured under the policy.


OUTCOME: The court entered judgment in favor of the driver.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Insurance -- automobile liability policy of person who patronized restaurant with private parking lot covers attendant there who, at such insured patron's request, drove latter's car out to him and accidentally injured another person.

1. A liability policy insured the owner of an automobile and his spouse and "any other person or organization legally responsible for its use, provided the actual use of the automobile is by the named insured or spouse or with the permission of either"; but the policy nevertheless excluded "any person * * * employed in or operating an automobile business"; and it defined "automobile business" as "an automobile repair shop, public garage, sales agency, service station or public parking place." The named insured visited a restaurant and drove his car to an adjacent parking lot maintained by the restaurant for the use of its patrons only. The restaurant permitted plaintiff, on evenings and weekends, to act as parking attendant, without compensation other than customers' gratuities. The named insured permitted plaintiff to take his car, at the entrance to the parking lot, and to drive it into the lot; and later, when the insured emerged from the [***2] restaurant, the insured asked plaintiff to get the car, whereupon plaintiff proceeded to drive the insured's car back to the entrance of the parking lot and while so doing was involved in an accident which caused another person to suffer personal injuries. The injured person sued plaintiff. Plaintiff sued the insurance company and is awarded judgment declaring that he is an insured whom the insurance company must defend.

2. Within the meaning of the policy, this was not a "public" parking place.

3. Even if a policy can fairly be construed both favorably to the insurance company and to the insured, the burden is on the insurance company to demonstrate that the construction in its favor is the only fair one.

 

2508 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


New Edgewood Properties, Inc. v. Sachsman


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


City Court of New York, New York, New York County


22 Misc. 2d 36; 194 N.Y.S.2d 186; 1959 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2558



November 24, 1959 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The motion is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff landlord brought an action against defendant tenant for rent and insurance premiums. The tenant filed a motion to dismiss the complaint, relying on N.Y. R. Civ. Prac. 301(5) (1953) and claiming that res judicata barred the action because the landlord brought two previous actions that were voluntarily dismissed.


OVERVIEW: The landlord's first action for rent was dismissed for lack of jurisdiction by a justice of the peace. The landlord discontinued the second action after a discussion between the parties about the invalidity of the service of process. In considering the matter, the court ruled that in view of the drastic consequences of a discontinuance filed in the early stages of an action, N.Y. R. Civ. Prac. 301(5) did not apply to a second and voluntary discontinuance of an action that was brought in a court without jurisdiction to hear the case on the merits. The court further ruled that a party was barred from proceeding again only when he brought an action a second time, without reference to the first, in a court with jurisdiction and then discontinued the action. The court determined that in the prior action in the same court, there was no jurisdiction over the tenant. The discontinuance of that action, therefore, merely anticipated the tenant's motion to dismiss. The tenant also prevented the matter from being heard by appearing only specially to contest jurisdiction. The court thus concluded that the landlord had not had the second opportunity given him by the rule to prove his case.


OUTCOME: The court denied the tenant's motion to dismiss the complaint.


HEADNOTES

Dismissal and nonsuit -- discontinuance "on the merits" (Rules Civ. Prac., rule 301, subd. 5) does not apply where second voluntary discontinuance is of action brought in court which lacked jurisdiction to hear case on merits.

1. Rule 301 (subd. 5) of the Rules of Civil Practice, providing that a discontinuance shall be regarded as on the merits, does not apply to a second and voluntary discontinuance of an action brought in a court which did not have jurisdiction to hear the case on the merits. Here, the second discontinuance followed a special appearance by defendant to set aside service as invalid where the court did not have jurisdiction of the person of defendant. Plaintiff did not have a second opportunity to prove his case and a motion to dismiss the complaint is denied.

2. Plaintiff will not be required to pay defendant's attorneys' fees and expenses in order to proceed. Defendant has a remedy if he feels himself unjustly harassed.

 

2509 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Vosburg


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


County Court of New York, Schenectady County


21 Misc. 2d 372; 193 N.Y.S.2d 158; 1959 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2593



November 19, 1959 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The indictment is dismissed.

Enter order accordingly.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant resigned from public office. He was called before the grand jury, was sworn, and testified in an investigation entitled "People of the State of New York against John Doe." The grand jury indicted defendant for conspiracy, larceny, and other crimes. Defendant filed a motion to dismiss the indictment, pursuant to N.Y. Const. art. I, § 6 and the Fifth Amendment, on grounds that he had been compelled to testify against himself.


OVERVIEW: Defendant refused to sign a waiver of immunity before the grand jury and on certain matters, he refused to testify on constitutional grounds. The grand jury indicted defendant. Defendant filed a motion to dismiss the indictment. The State contended that defendant had not been indicted in connection with any matter on which he had testified, and that what testimony he had given in no way furnished any link in the chain of evidence that resulted in the indictment. The court held that defendant had been compelled to testify against himself. From a reading of the record, there was little doubt that the investigation had been specifically directed against defendant. While defendant had refused to answer some questions, the very form of a question put to defendant that asked him whether he had committed an improper act, followed by his refusal to answer, might have prejudiced defendant in the eyes of the grand jury, and so have contributed to the finding of the indictment. Certainly, defendant had been sworn and had testified, both generally and specifically, as to the matters under investigation and affecting him, which violated N.Y. Const. art. I, § 6 and the Fifth Amendment.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed the indictment.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- privilege against self incrimination -- defendant, who was target of investigation, was called and examined before Grand Jury -- indictment followed -- privilege against self incrimination was violated -- protection from indictment and use of incriminating testimony automatically results -- indictment dismissed.

The day after defendant resigned as Commissioner of Public Welfare of the County of Schenectady he was called before the Grand Jury as a witness in an investigation dealing with the conduct of the office of the Commissioner of Public Welfare of said county and with his actions while holding that office. He was sworn and testified, both generally and specifically, as to the matters under investigation and affecting him. He thus became a witness against himself. Following his testimony an indictment against him was found by the Grand Jury which heard him testify. Where one who is a target of an investigation is called and examined before a Grand Jury has constitutionally conferred privilege against self incrimination is violated and protection from indictment and from use of any incriminating testimony [***2] given by him automatically results. The indictment against the defendant is, therefore, dismissed.

 

2510 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Meyers v. Cornwall


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Orange County


24 Misc. 2d 286; 192 N.Y.S.2d 734; 1959 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2699



November 4, 1959 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The stay contained in the order to show cause issued in this action on July 29, 1959 is vacated.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff, the owner and operator of a dump, filed an action seeking an adjudication declaring that Cornwall-on-Hudson, N.Y., Ordinance Regulating Dumps (1959) was unconstitutional and void, and an injunction restraining the enforcement of said ordinance, against defendant town, which moved to dismiss the complaint for legal insufficiency.


OVERVIEW: The owner contended that the ordinance was not a proper, reasonable, and valid exercise of the town's police power; that it was vague, indefinite, and speculative in its terms and provisions; that in applying the test of future possibilities in the operation of the ordinance, the town was permitted to act in such an arbitrary and unreasonable manner as to violate the constitutional rights of the owner and others similarly situated in the conduct of his and their business of maintaining refuse and garbage dumps; and that § V of the ordinance violated the requirements of due process. The court found the first three arguments to have been without merit, but sustained the fourth. The court found § V of the ordinance violated the rule against presumptive devices in criminal enactments where it provided that the holder of a permit would be personally responsible for the condition of the dump site or any condition created elsewhere as a result of the condition of the dump site, and that it would be conclusively presumed that the holder of a permit had knowledge of any condition that was noxious or offensive within 24 hours after its occurrence.


OUTCOME: The court granted the town a judgment on the merits, but without costs, and declared that the ordinance was valid in all respects except for the provisions of § V thereof which were declared invalid.


HEADNOTES

Municipal corporations -- ordinance regulating dumps -- respondent town had authority, in exercise of police power and pursuant to subdivision 6 of section 130 of Town Law, to adopt ordinance regulating disposition of garbage -- provisions are sufficiently clear, definite and certain -- provision that board may impose "reasonable and necessary" additional conditions in granting permit sets up proper standard for conduct of board -- section V of ordinance, providing that holder of permit shall be conclusively presumed to have knowledge of existence of noxious or offensive conditions at, or caused by, dump, violates due process requirement and is unconstitutional -- section V excluded as invalid -- balance of subject ordinance sustained.

1. Respondent town had the authority, in the exercise of its police power, to adopt an ordinance regulating the collection and disposition of refuse and garbage (Town Law, § 130, subd. 6). Where, as here, the ordinance has a substantial relationship to a threat to public health and safety, the regulations contained therein may not be said to [***2] be unreasonable, arbitrary, capricious, discriminatory or oppressive.

2. The licensing provisions and purposes of the subject ordinance are sufficiently clear, definite and certain to afford a comprehensible guide as to what must be done or avoided to comply with the requirements.

3. A provision that the board, in passing upon a permit for the maintenance of a dump, may impose such additional terms and conditions as it deems "necessary and proper" sets up a proper standard for the conduct of the board. Even were no standard set down, unfair and unreasonable action is subject to judicial review (Civ. Prac. Act, art. 78).

4. However, section V of the ordinance of defendant town "regulating dumps", providing that the holder of such a permit shall be personally responsible for the condition of such dump site and shall be conclusively presumed to have knowledge of noxious or offensive conditions at, or caused by, said dump site, would deprive an alleged violator of due process of law. As the ordinance provides criminal, as well as civil, penalties, said provision is unconstitutional and is excluded from the ordinance.

5. In view of the foregoing, the motion to dismiss the complaint [***3] for legal insufficiency is neither granted nor denied. Judgment is granted in favor of defendant, declaring the challenged ordinance valid in all respects except that said section V is declared invalid. Defendant may not be enjoined from enforcing said ordinance; the stay contained in the order to show cause herein is vacated; and plaintiff is given 21 days in which to apply for the necessary permit.
