People v. Stevens


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


County Court of New York, Jefferson County


16 Misc. 2d 162; 183 N.Y.S.2d 968; 1959 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 4278



February 18, 1959 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of the decision of the Court of Special Sessions, Jefferson County (New York), which convicted him of failing to have a car under control in violation of N.Y. Veh. & Traf. Law § 56(1) and of resisting arrest by a police officer in violation of N.Y. Penal Law § 1825 upon defendant's pleas of guilty.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was arrested for failure to have a car under control in violation of N.Y. Veh. & Traf. Law § 56(1). Defendant was also charged with resisting arrest in violation of N.Y. Penal Law § 1825 for an incident that occurred while he was being apprehended for the traffic charge. The information charged defendant with "sideswiping" a car, going "to the left side" and driving so as to endanger others. Defendant pleaded guilty to both charges and was convicted by the trial court. On appeal, the court reversed and held that N.Y. Veh. & Traf. Law § 56(1) was invalid at the time of defendant's arrest because other courts had found it to be unconstitutional. The court found that even if § 56(1) was not invalid, the information was defective because it failed to show circumstances that could have differentiated an ordinary accident and civil negligence from criminal liability despite the plea of guilty. The court noted that defendant did not waive his objection that the information did not charge a crime when he pleaded guilty. The court held that defendant's conviction under N.Y. Penal Law § 1825 was invalid because it was based on his arrest for the invalid traffic charge.


OUTCOME: The court reversed defendant's convictions of failing to have a car under control and resisting arrest. The court dismissed the information against defendant.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- resisting arrest on charge of violating unconstitutional law -- subdivision 1 of section 56 of Vehicle and Traffic Law being unconstitutional, arrest was invalid; hence defendant's convictions, on his pleas of guilty, of violating said section and of resisting arrest (Penal Law; § 1825), are reversed and informations dismissed.

Subdivision 1 of section 56 of the Vehicle and Traffic Law being unconstitutional, an arrest for a violation of said statute was invalid; hence defendant's convictions, on his pleas of guilty, of violating said section and of resisting arrest (Penal Law, § 1825), are reversed and the informations dismissed on the law, the defendant discharged, and the sureties exonerated.

 

2554 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Fra-Nat Builders, Inc. v. Oyster Bay


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Nassau County


16 Misc. 2d 851; 183 N.Y.S.2d 830; 1959 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 4303



February 16, 1959 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff property owner brought an action for a judgment declaring unconstitutional and invalid a zoning ordinance that made the subject property a residential area. The property owner claimed that the highest and best use of the property was for business use.


OVERVIEW: The subject property was bound on three of its four sides by residences. The only break in the residential quality of the immediate vicinity was a shopping center and the farm-like garden center and greenhouse to the north of the subject property. The property owner presented evidence that the best use of the property was for business purposes. The court held that the complaint was subject to dismissal because zoning ordinances were not required to accommodate the highest and best use of property. It was sufficient if the zoning ordinance permitted a reasonable use. The property owner failed to show that the zoning was arbitrary, capricious, or unreasonable just because a better or more profitable use was possible.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed the complaint.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Municipal corporations -- zoning -- even if best use of premises is for business, residential zoning is reasonable and constitutional.

Even if the testimony adduced on behalf of plaintiff, to the effect that the best use of his residential-zoned premises would be for business, were accepted, his action to declare such residential zoning unconstitutional as to his premises must be dismissed. His property is bounded on three sides by residences. The only break in the residential quality of the immediate vicinity is by a shopping center and a farm-like garden center and greenhouse to the north of his property. The due process clauses of the Constitutions do not require that a landowner be afforded the best use of his property; they merely require that he be afforded reasonable use. What is a reasonable use, for zoning purposes, is a legislative matter. The party assailing legislation must show its unconstitutionality beyond a reasonable doubt.

 

2555 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Warshaw v. Jacobs


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Queens County


16 Misc. 2d 844; 183 N.Y.S.2d 939; 1959 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 4475



January 22, 1959 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Petition dismissed. Submit order.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, the owners and licensees of a private proprietary hospital, brought a proceeding under N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 78 to review and annul the determination of respondent Hospital Board which denied their application for a variance from the provisions of the Hospital Code and Regulations, and denied their application for a renewal and issuance of a license to operate the hospital.


OVERVIEW: The licensees asserted that new Hospital Code and Regulations which required each hospital room to have at least 70 square feet and certain footage between beds was in violation of the Federal and State Constitutions in that they are in deprivation of property without due process of law. The court dismissed their petition, holding that they failed to allege facts which may establish the Board's determination and refusal as arbitrary, capricious or unreasonable. They had no vested right in the continuation of their hospital on a 50-bed basis. For the Board to grant a variance they were required to show unique circumstances or that they suffered a singular disadvantage or unnecessary hardship through the operation of the amended code and regulations. No such foundation for a variance was alleged.


OUTCOME: The licensees petition to annul the determination of the Hospital Board was dismissed.


HEADNOTES

Hospitals -- licenses -- under Code and Regulations of New York City Hospital Board, private proprietary hospitals may constitutionally be required to have certain footage between beds, so that petitioner hospital is now reduced from 50 beds to 29 beds.

In New York City, private proprietary hospitals are licensed for one year and are subject to the city's Hospital Board's Code and Regulations (New York City Charter, § 583-a, subd. 2). Such a hospital was licensed to operate 50 beds; but a subsequent amendment to said code and regulations required at least 70 square feet and certain footage between beds, so that the hospital could thereafter be licensed for only 29 beds. Said hospital's application to the city's Hospital Board for a variance on the ground of hardship or unique circumstances was denied. In this review proceeding (Civ. Prac. Act, art. 78) the hospital has not shown that such denial was arbitrary or unreasonable or confiscatory or unconstitutional. The proceeding is dismissed.

 

2556 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Bresett v. Ogdensburg


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Franklin County


14 Misc. 2d 912; 180 N.Y.S.2d 908; 1958 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2063



December 31, 1958 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] In the exercise of discretion, the motion for a temporary injunction is granted.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff property owner sought a temporary injunction against defendant city and its agencies in an action seeking to declare void and unconstitutional a resolution of the city's common council that had granted the city a variance to use property, adjacent to the owner's, for sanitary land-fill operations.


OVERVIEW: The property was zoned for residential use under the city's existing zoning ordinance and the property owner had been developing several tracts accordingly. The city contended that, because the parcel in question had been used for sand-pit purposes prior to adoption of the zoning ordinance, such nonconforming use was an industrial use, which under the ordinance could be extended to include sanitary fill operations. The property owner argued that the city and its agencies, in granting the variance or the permission to extend the nonconforming use, acted illegally because there had been no notice to residents and because no evidence was taken upon which the council's conclusions of law and fact were made. In granting the temporary injunction, the court found that, although the city could take actions permitted by the zoning ordinance and by N.Y. Gen. City Law § 20(24) and (25), such actions could not be arbitrary or capricious. The concluded that the city and its agencies were required to fully disclose the facts and the bases that supported the decision to grant the use variance or the permission to extend the parcel's nonconforming use.


OUTCOME: The court, in the exercise of discretion, granted the property owner's motion for a temporary injunction against the city and ordered that the trial of the action be preferred, subject to the approval of the presiding judge.


HEADNOTES

Municipal corporations -- zoning -- owner of property in residential zone is granted temporary injunction against city which took lease of prior nonconforming sandpit and obtained variance to use it as dump for sanitary land-fill operations by resolution of Common Council sitting as Board of Appeals and acting on its own knowledge.

Plaintiff owns improved land in a residential zone. A certain parcel in that zone had a prior nonconforming sandpit use, which would be an industrial zone use. The city recently took a lease of said premises, and was granted a variance and extension of said prior nonconforming use, by the city's Common Council sitting as a zoning Board of Appeals, to use said premises as a dump for sanitary land-fill operations, such determination being based on unstated facts known only to the board members personally ( General City Law, § 82, subd. 3; § 20, subd. 25). In this action to invalidate the variance as unconstitutional and void, plaintiff is granted an injunction pendente lite.

 

2557 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Scarscale Supply Co. v. Scarsdale


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Westchester County


15 Misc. 2d 289; 181 N.Y.S.2d 374; 1958 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2065



December 30, 1958 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff supply company filed an action for a declaratory judgment in which it sought to hold a 1955 rezoning ordinance of defendant Village of Scarsdale, N.Y., unconstitutional. It also sought a declaration that the ordinance was confiscatory and void insofar as it affected its property, which had been reclassified from a business district to a residence district. The supply company continued to operate its supply yard on the property.


OVERVIEW: In October 1955, by amendment to the zoning ordinance, the property was placed in the newly created residence district, which no longer permitted the operation of a building supply yard. The court first noted that the use had becoming a nonconforming use and that no one was questioning the fact that the supply company now lawfully used and could continue to operate the supply yard. The supply company contended that in adopting the 1955 rezoning, the village failed to comply with N.Y. Village Law § 179. The court found the contention to be without merit. The rezoning was subsequently made effective by the enactment of a new village code in 1957 that specifically repealed all previously enacted ordinances and enacted new ones, including the zoning provisions applicable to the supply company's property. The court also found that it was difficult to see how the supply company was presently aggrieved or deprived in any manner of the use of its property. Before the court could consider whether the Village could restrict, without compensation, the use of the property, there should be proof that the 1957 village code diminished the value of the present property rights.


OUTCOME: Finding that the supply company had failed to prove the allegations of its complaint by a fair preponderance of the credible evidence, the court dismissed the complaint.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Municipal corporations -- zoning -- property which concededly has prior nonconforming use cannot attack constitutionality of zoning reclassification from business use to residential use.

1. A property owner who concededly has the right to continue his pre-existing nonconforming use of his property and buildings as a building supply yard cannot maintain an action against the village for a judgment declaring that an amendment to the zoning ordinance and an entirely new zoning ordinance have unreasonably, confiscatorily and unconstitutionally reclassified his property from a business district to a residence district. The village does not question his right to continue to use his property as a building supply yard. He has shown that, under the former business zone classification, he could also have profitably converted his property to use as a shopping or retail store center; but he has not shown any present intent to undertake any such conversion. Nor is there any proof that the village contemplates using its power to enact legislation requiring nonconforming uses to be discontinued within a reasonable and nonconfiscatory time. In short, there is no present controversy, and [***2] the complaint is dismissed.

2. In 1955, by amendment to its zoning ordinance, the village placed plaintiff's property in a newly created residence district, instead of its former business district. Section 179 of the Village Law, as it then read, required that, in case of protest against a proposed change in zoning signed by the owners of 20% or more of the area of the land included in the proposed change, "such amendment shall not become effective except by the favorable vote of all the members of the board of trustees" Plaintiff had filed a written protest, and plaintiff's property constituted more than 20% of the area involved by the creation of the new residence district. The amendment was adopted by a merely 5-to-2 vote of the village's Board of Trustees. However, shortly before adopting the amendment, the village had enacted a local law (Local Laws, 1955, No. 4 of Village of Scarsdale) substituting a requirement for a merely two-thirds vote instead of the unanimity required by section 179 of the Village Law. This local law may have been authorized by the Village Home Rule Law (§ 11, subd. 4). Incidentally, section 179 of the Village Law was amended in 1956 (L. 1956, ch.  [***3] 759) to require only a three-fourths vote of a board of trustees. In any event, in 1957 the village Board of Trustees by unanimous vote repealed the zoning ordinance and its amendments and enacted a new zoning ordinance (Scarsdale Village Code, 1957) placing plaintiff's property in a residence district. This new, unanimous ordinance is unquestionably valid. The rights of plaintiff must be determined by the valid law as it exists today.

 

2558 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Jokinen v. Allen


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Nassau County


15 Misc. 2d 124; 182 N.Y.S.2d 166; 1958 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2088



December 23, 1958 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Summary judgment is granted to the defendants dismissing every other portion of the complaint ( Wohl v. Miller, 5 A D 2d 126, 133, 134).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, operators of nonpublic kindergartens, filed a motion for summary judgment in their action seeking to declare as unconstitutional § 195 of the "Regulations of the Commissioner," adopted by defendant, the Board of Regents of the State of New York on March 1, 1957. Defendants, the Board and the Commissioner of Education of the State of New York, filed a cross-motion for summary judgment dismissing the complaint.


OVERVIEW: Section 195 provided for the registration of nonpublic kindergartens. The operators alleged that the registration program was designed to influence the public not to deal with unregistered schools. The court found that a provision in a bulletin concerning the admission of children from registered programs into first grade unlawfully discriminated against children from unregistered programs. Accordingly, the operators were entitled to partial summary judgment. The court held, however, that it was within the power of the Board and Commissioner to enact the remainder of the rules of the Board and the "general regulations" of the Commissioner. Section 195 furnished adequate standards for the Commissioner put into effect the educational policies of the State. N.Y. Educ. Law § 207 was sufficiently broad to encompass the institution by the Board of a noncompulsory registration program. The court determined that the rules and regulations were not arbitrary or capricious, or improper, but rather, were reasonable. The operators were not so aggrieved to maintain the action as they had not shown that their legal rights were infringed or that they were denied equal protection.


OUTCOME: The court granted partial summary judgment in favor of the operators to the extent that the Commissioner's direction, which gave a preference to children from registered private kindergartens in admission to the first grade of a public school, was discriminatory, improper, and a violation of the constitutional rights of others. The court granted summary judgment to the Board and the Commissioner dismissing every other portion of the complaint.


HEADNOTES

Schools -- registration of private kindergartens -- Board of Regents adopted section 195 of Regulations of Commissioner providing that nonpublic kindergartens might be registered by Education Department upon compliance with stated conditions -- in action by operators of private kindergartens for judgment declaring such section and implementing regulations of Education Commissioner unconstitutional, plaintiffs were awarded summary judgment declaring that direction of Commissioner, which gave preference in admission to first grade in public schools to children who attended registered private kindergartens, was unconstitutional and defendants were awarded summary judgment dismissing balance of complaint.

1. The Board of Regents adopted, on March 1, 1957, section 195 of the Regulations of the Commissioner of Education, and on the same day the Commissioner of Education adopted "General Regulations" to implement section 195. Section 195 provided that a nonpublic nursery school and/or kindergarten might be registered, on a voluntary [***2] basis, by the Education Department if it should present evidence of compliance with stated conditions relating to buildings and equipment, personnel, curriculum, teacher-pupil ratio, etc. The Regulations of the Commissioner set up the mechanism of registration, supplemented the Regulations of the Board of Regents and provided that when a school had been registered it should receive from the Education Department, a "Certificate of Approval" which might be displayed on its premises as long as it was valid. A bulletin of the Commissioner of Education dated August 1958, as interpreted by the court, provided that local public school authorities must admit to the first grade any child who attended and satisfactorily completed a full year's work at a registered nonpublic kindergarten, if it were 5 years, 8 months or more on September 1st, upon entering the first grade and that they might not admit a child of the same age to first grade upon completion of a full year at an unregistered nonpublic kindergarten unless and until they determined that such unregistered kindergarten provided instruction substantially equivalent to that of the local public school. If such kindergarten did provide [***3] such instruction, "the child should be placed in the first grade." In this action by operators of nursery schools and kindergartens for judgment declaring section 195 of the Regulations of the Commissioner and the General Regulations unconstitutional, summary judgment was granted plaintiffs to the extent of declaring that the direction of defendant Commissioner of Education which gave preference to children from registered private kindergartens in admission to the first grade of a public school was unconstitutional, and summary judgment was granted defendants dismissing every other portion of the complaint.

2. Section 207 of the Education Law conferred upon defendant Board of Regents power to "exercise legislative functions concerning the educational system of the state, determine its educational policies, and, except, as to the judicial functions of the commissioner of education, establish rules for carrying into effect the laws and policies of the state, relating to education, and the functions, powers, duties and trusts conferred or charged upon the university and the education department." Such broad delegation of power furnished authority for the enactment of section 195 of the [***4] Regulations of the Commissioner of Education. Section 207 of the Education Law was not directly attacked in the complaint, and was presumed to be valid. Its constitutionality may not be passed upon when the issue had not been raised by the pleadings. Having failed to do so, plaintiffs could not question it by memorandum of law or by oral argument.

3. The statute was sufficiently broad to encompass the institution by the Board of Regents of a noncompulsory registration program for private nursery schools and kindergartens.

4. Section 195 provided both general and specific standards to limit and define the powers and duties of the Commissioner of Education. He adopted a set of rules to carry out the intent of the Board of Regents as expressed in that enactment. These rules, with the exception of the provision concerning admission to first grade from nonpublic kindergartens, were a proper implementation of section 195 by the executive officer charged with its administration. Both the delegation of authority and the exercise of that authority were warranted under the circumstances.

5. The rules and regulations were a reasonable exercise of defendants' powers to determine and put [***5] into effect the educational policies of the State and were not arbitrary or capricious.

6. Plaintiffs have not been denied the equal protection of the law.

7. The registration program was purely voluntary. The complaint contained no factual allegations to support the charge that the rules were designed to coerce plaintiffs into conformity with the department's standards, or to place in a more favorable competitive position those who did comply with such standards. In the absence of positive proof it might not be presumed the motives of part of the Department of Education were improper. If, conceivably, some parents might misinterpret the absence of a Department of Education certificate as evidence of inferiority, defendants could not be held legally responsible for this false conclusion. Plaintiffs were free to correct this error by any proper means they choose.

8. No illegal force, coercion or duress was applied. No discrimination was shown nor did it appear that any other constitutional right of these plaintiffs was violated.

 

2559 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Parmer Realty Corp. v. Bercowitz


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Municipal Court of the City of New York, Borough of Brooklyn


15 Misc. 2d 397; 180 N.Y.S.2d 987; 1958 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2161



December 16, 1958 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Final order is made in favor of the tenant dismissing the landlord's petition, with costs of this proceeding.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff landlord brought an action to evict defendant tenant from a three-room basement apartment. The department of housing and buildings placed a violation against the premises because of the occupancy of the apartment by the tenant, who was a person other than a janitor and his family as limited by the provisions of N.Y. Mult. Dwell. Law § 216(2)(e). The tenant filed an answer.


OVERVIEW: The tenant's defense was that the provisions of § 216 limiting occupancy of the basement apartment solely to a janitor and his family was discriminatory and unconstitutional. The court found no difficulty in holding that the general purpose of § 216 and its subdivisions dealing with the proper housing safeguards, sanitation, and light were in furtherance of legislative aims to protect the public welfare. However, the specific provision of § 216(2)(e) limiting the use of such a basement apartment solely to a janitor and his family presented another problem. The court found that the only proper consideration was the safety and welfare of the occupants regardless of their occupation. It was obvious that the apartment adequately met proper housing standards for human habitation. Considering the present shortage of apartments and the fact that a final order issued in favor of the landlord would of necessity direct the issuance of a warrant evicting the tenant, the court declared that so much of § 216(2)(e) as directed that the basement apartment created thereunder should be occupied by a superintendent and his family only was discriminatory and unconstitutional.


OUTCOME: The court made a final order in favor of the tenant dismissing the landlord's petition.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional law -- Multiple Dwelling Law -- statutory limitation (Multiple Dwelling Law, § 216, subd. 2, par. e) that apartment which complies with statutory standards may be occupied only by janitor and his family is discriminatory and unconstitutional; landlord is not entitled to evict tenant of such apartment who is not janitor.

Paragraph e of subdivision 2 of section 216 of the Multiple Dwelling Law limits the use of certain basement apartments to a janitor and his family. Although the general purposes of section 216 are within the furtherance of the aims expressed by the Legislature, as they relate to proper housing safeguards, sanitation, and light, so much of the subdivision as limits the occupancy of an apartment, which does comply with the standards, to a janitor and his family is discriminatory and unconstitutional. Accordingly, landlord's petition to evict the tenant who is not a janitor from such an apartment, because the Department of Housing and Buildings has placed a violation against the premises as a result of this tenant's occupancy, is [***2] dismissed.

 

2560 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


In re Skipwith


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Domestic Relations Court of the City of New York, Children's Court Division, New York County


14 Misc. 2d 325; 180 N.Y.S.2d 852; 1958 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2163



December 15, 1958 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The petitions are dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner board of education charged respondent parents with neglect of their children, alleging that the children lacked proper guardianship because the parents refused to send the children to their assigned schools or to provide substitute teaching in compliance with the law. The parents defended on the ground that the children were denied equal education opportunities in violation of U.S. Const. amend XIV.


OVERVIEW: In response to petitions filed by the school board for alleged neglect, the parents asserted, in justification of their refusal to send their children to assigned schools, that the schools offered educationally inferior opportunities as a result of de facto racial segregation and discriminatory teacher staffing. The court dismissed the petitions, holding that N.Y. Educ. Law § 310 was not a bar to a judicial determination of whether the board of education violated legal rights, whatever their origin and the defense of inferior educational opportunities in the schools by reason of racial discrimination was established and barred an adjudication of neglect. The record sustained the parents' contention that the children had inferior educational opportunities where the schools were attended predominantly by minority children who were taught by less qualified teachers. The separation of children by race, whether the result of governmental action or private housing segregation, created factors inimical to full and equal educational opportunities. Further, teachers who exercised the board of education's power for teacher assignments were not acting in matters of merely private concern.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed the petitions by the board of education against the parents.


HEADNOTES

Schools -- racial segregation -- parents cannot constitutionally be compelled to send their children to schools which are racially segregated and relatively poorly staffed, even if school segregation is result of neighborhood segregation not caused by governmental action, and even if equal staffing would necessitate large sums and efforts by city.

The Board of Education of the City of New York cannot constitutionally invoke the sanctions of the courts against Negro parents and against Puerto Rican parents who refuse to send their children to two schools in which there are no white children and in which half of the teachers are not regularly licensed teachers but are substitute teachers with relatively inferior qualifications and educational attainments. There is no evidence in this case that the racial segregation in these schools is caused by any circumstances other than residential segregation in the city which is not attributable to any governmental action. Nevertheless, children are entitled to equal educational opportunities, and they and their parents are entitled to the "equal protection of the laws" (U. S. Const., 14th Amdt.), even [***2] if the establishment of such equal opportunities would require large expenditures of money and effort by the city. In view of this provision of the Constitution, the parents need not obtain for their children the "required instruction elsewhere" (Education Law, § 3212, subd. 2, par. d), although the parents in this case have in fact arranged for some private tutoring; nor can the parents be relegated to proceeding before the State Commissioner of Education (Education Law, § 310). The court will not adjudge that the children are "neglected" or are "unlawfully kept out of school", and the court will not sanction proceedings which might result in imprisonment of the parents therefor (N. Y. City Dom. Rel. Ct. Act, § 2, subd. [17]; § 61, subd. 1; § 83). These two proceedings by the Board of Education are dismissed.

 

2561 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Baer v. Kolmorgen


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Westchester County


14 Misc. 2d 1015; 181 N.Y.S.2d 230; 1958 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2169



December 15, 1958 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Complaint dismissed, with costs. Settle judgment on notice.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff taxpayers brought a lawsuit against defendant school board seeking a declaratory judgment and an injunction prohibiting the display of a nativity scene on school property during the school's Christmas vacation.


OVERVIEW: The taxpayers filed a lawsuit against the board to prevent the erection of a nativity scene on school grounds by a multi-denominational religious group. The taxpayers sought a permanent injunction and declaratory judgment. On hearing, the court dismissed the taxpayers' complaint, with costs, and found that only one taxpayer, the only one whose child actually attended the school at issue, had standing to maintain the action against the board. As to the one legitimate claimant, the court found no danger of irreparable injury, no evidence of public financing, and no evidence of increased cost to the school or to any taxpayer. The court held that the board's actions in allowing the religious symbols were a mere accommodation, not unlike accommodations made for other religious and non-religious groups, and that those actions were not unconstitutional under the First Amendment. The court found that religious symbols are inescapable during the Christmas season and that no greater religious influence was imposed upon students by the display during the school vacation on public property than that imposed by the religious symbols displayed on private property at that time of year.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed the taxpayers' lawsuit, with costs, after considering the request for declaratory judgment and denied the injunctive relief for lack of any proof of permanent harm or damage. The court found that the board's approval of the display was a mere accommodation and not a constitutional violation.


HEADNOTES

Schools -- Nativity Scene exhibit on lawn at Christmas -- action for declaratory judgment and injunction against school board's permitting private committee to erect and display Nativity Scene on school lawn during Christmas holidays, without involving school funds or personnel, is dismissed.

1. An action to restrain a school board from permitting a Nativity Scene to be erected and displayed on the lawn of the high school during the Christmas holidays, on the ground that such permission violates the State Constitution (art. I, § 3) and the United States Constitution (First Amdt. and Fourteenth Amdt.), is maintainable only by a person who is not only a taxpayer of the school district but is also the parent of a child attending this particular high school at the time of the act complained of. A general taxpayer's action (General Municipal Law, § 51) is not maintainable against a board of education.

2. Only one of the plaintiffs in this suit is thus a proper plaintiff, and he has failed to show irreparable injury, hence the suit should be dismissed.

3. Moreover, the Nativity Scene or Creche has been removed,  [***2] and no application for another display is pending before the school board; hence there is nothing to enjoin and the complaint should be dismissed for that reason.

4. However, the court will consider the complaint on the merits insofar as it seeks a declaratory judgment, since the controversy appears to be renewed on Christmas after Christmas.

5. The evidence establishes that no public funds or time of public employees were expended, and that even the electricity was paid for by a private committee; and, furthermore, the display was on view during school holidays only, and no school instruction was given at any time as to the meaning of the display. The accommodation or permission granted by the school board to the private Creche committee did not violate constitutional provisions (N. Y. Const., art. I, § 3; U. S. Const., First and Fourteenth Amdts.). The complaint is dismissed.

 

2562 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Herkel v. Kelly


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Oneida County


14 Misc. 2d 966; 180 N.Y.S.2d 353; 1958 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2245



December 4, 1958 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The petition is therefore dismissed.

Order accordingly.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner licensee filed a petition under N.Y. Civ. Prac. Act art. 78 to set aside an order issued by respondent Commissioner of Motor Vehicles (New York) temporarily suspending the licensee's operator's license pending a hearing on a scheduled date.


OVERVIEW: After the licensee pleaded guilty to operating a motor vehicle upon a public highway at a speed of in excess of 95 miles per hour, the Commissioner temporarily suspended the licensee's operator's license pending a hearing. The court dismissed the licensee's petition to set aside the Commissioner's order, holding that: (1) the temporary suspension was reviewable under N.Y. Civ. Prac. Act art. 78 because it was a final determination of the right of the licensee to operate his automobile during the period of the temporary suspension and N.Y. Veh. & Traf. Law § 71(6) provided that suspension was an administrative act reviewable by a New York supreme court; (2) § 71(3)(e), which authorized the temporary suspension pending a hearing, was not unconstitutional because the licensee was not deprived of his license without due process of law where relief was available under N.Y. Civ. Prac. Act art. 78; and (3) the Commissioner's action was not arbitrary or capricious because the restriction of 26 days imposed upon the licensee was not unreasonable and the record contained ample evidence to justify temporary suspension pending the scheduled hearing.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed the licensee's petition to set aside the Commissioner's order.


HEADNOTES

Motor vehicles -- temporary suspension of license -- order of temporary suspension is reviewable as final order -- authority of commissioner to suspend license pending hearing is constitutional as reasonable regulation of highways -- unreasonably long temporary suspension would be arbitrary but 26 days in this case was not unreasonable (Vehicle and Traffic Law, § 71, subd. 3, par. [e]; subd. 6).

1. Petitioner was arrested for violation of the Vehicle and Traffic Law on August 27, 1958, and pleaded guilty. On November 14, 1958, he received a notice from respondent State Commissioner of Motor Vehicles that a hearing was scheduled for December 10, 1958, and that his license was suspended pending the hearing, according to the Vehicle and Traffic Law ( § 71, subd. 3, par. [e]), which authorizes such temporary suspensions without notice pending a hearing for gross negligence. This temporary suspension may be reviewed both because of section 71 (subd. 6) under which revocation or suspension is deemed a reviewable administrative act and also because the determination is a final one of the rights of petitioner to operate [***2] his automobile during the temporary period. However, the suspension was amply justified in this case.

2. The statute is constitutional, in authorizing suspension without notice pending a hearing, as a reasonable regulation of the highways. As the court will interpret the section to mean that a temporary suspension be for a reasonable time, the provision is not unconstitutional merely because it does not contain a specific time limit.

3. An unreasonably long temporary suspension may be attacked as arbitrary and capricious, but the 26-day period in this case was not unreasonable, and the petition to set aside the order is therefore dismissed.

 

2563 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Atlantic Refining Co. v. Zoning Bd. of Appeals


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Erie County


14 Misc. 2d 1022; 180 N.Y.S.2d 656; 1958 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2265



December 2, 1958 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Proceeding dismissed upon the merits, without costs. Prepare and submit order accordingly.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner refining company brought a proceeding under N.Y. Civ. Prac. Act art. 78 for an order granting review of the determination of respondent village zoning board of appeals. The board denied the company's appeal from a decision of a village board denying it permission to erect a gasoline service station in the village.


OVERVIEW: The company brought an action challenging the board's decision that declined to review the village zoning board's denial of permission to build a service station in the village. The board responded that the company was not a person aggrieved, the board had no jurisdiction to hear the appeal, the appeal was not timely, and the decision by the village zoning board was proper. The court found the only issue was whether the board's determination was prejudiced, arbitrary, capricious, or contrary to law. The court found that the company had no permit to build the station. Thus, the company had no vested right to the station. The village zoning board's determination was entitled to a presumption of correctness, and the company did not overcome that presumption. The company was allowed two separate hearings to present all evidence necessary to support its allegations, and it failed to fulfill its burden of proof. Therefore, the decision of the board to refuse to review the decision of the village zoning board was proper.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed the proceeding on the merits.


HEADNOTES

Municipal corporations -- zoning -- even after commencement of proceeding (Civ. Prac. Act, art. 78) to review denial of permit for garage and filling station in zone in which such permit was required, village could validly amend zoning ordinance so as to exclude any garage or filling station in such zone; contract vendee has no rights superior to village's power to amend its zoning ordinance; review proceeding dismissed -- although no record of any village zoning commission or ordinance under 1923 statute (Village Law, § 179-a), zoning ordinance in 1953 and amendment in 1958 are presumed valid.

1. A contract vendee of real property in a zone in which the village's 1953 zoning ordinance permitted "garages and filling stations, subject to such conditions and safeguards as deemed appropriate by the Board of Trustees" of the village, applied for such a permit in 1958. The Board of Trustees denied the application, and the village's Zoning Board of Appeals denied an appeal to it. The property owner commenced a review proceeding (Civ. Prac. Act, art. 78); and, shortly thereafter, the [***2] village amended its zoning ordinance so as not to allow any garages or filling stations in such zones. The court must decide the review proceeding upon the law as amended. Although a contract vendee may be "a person aggrieved" (Village Law, § 179-b), this one has no lawful grievance, since the zoning ordinance was validly amended. There were no vested rights here which were superior to the village's power to amend its zoning ordinance; nor was this applicant inequitably and prejudicially delayed by any official inaction. The review proceeding is dismissed on the merits.

2. Section 179-a of the Village Law (first added in 1923) required the board of trustees of a village to appoint a zoning commission to recommend the boundaries and uses of zones, before the village enacted any zoning ordinance. Petitioner, as a contract vendee of realty, avers that it searched the village records and found no record of any zoning ordinance enacted prior to 1923 and no record of any zoning commission, and that the first zoning ordinance and map was enacted by the village in 1953 and was therefore void. However, said 1953 zoning ordinance was enacted after a public hearing, and it expressly and [***3] validly repealed all former zoning ordinances. The zoning ordinance of 1953, and its 1958 amendment, are presumed valid, despite the failure to find any record of a zoning commission or of an earlier ordinance.

 

2564 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Conklin v. Wagner


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Kings County


15 Misc. 2d 978; 182 N.Y.S.2d 678; 1958 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2353



November 18, 1958 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants, the City of New York and members its Board of Estimate, sought an order to dismiss the complaint of plaintiff taxpayers for a declaratory judgment under N.Y. Gen. Mun. Law § 51, in which the taxpayers sought to have defendants' reports, consents, and resolutions declared null and void and requested a permanent injunction prohibiting defendants from taking any action on a proposed expressway.


OVERVIEW: Defendants claimed that the taxpayers' complaint was insufficient, because the City had the right to decide the approach that the expressway was to take to join a bridge and that the authority to make that decision was separate from the authorization to construct the bridge, which was a matter for the State. The taxpayers claimed that the State had preempted the decision regarding the location of the expressway by passing 1955 N.Y. Laws chs. 806-809, which provided the authority to construct the bridge. The court determined that Chapters 806-809 were to be read together and that the permission granted to the construct the bridge, which was the State's concern, was separate from the decision regarding the location of the expressway that was planned to join the bridge, which was a local matter for the City to determine under the provisions of N.Y. High. Law §§ 349-c(3.3) and 349-f (revised by 1955 N.Y. Laws ch. 809).


OUTCOME: The court granted defendants' motion to dismiss the taxpayers' complaint for insufficiency.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Municipal corporations -- home rule -- taxpayers' action seeking to restrain City of New York from approving approach to proposed Narrows Bridge and from appropriating money therefor and from exercising eminent domain therefor, dismissed (Highway Law, § 349-c, subd. 3.3; § 349-f, as amd. by L. 1955, ch. 809; N. Y. Const., art. IX, § 11).

Under the Highway Law ( § 349-c, subd. 3.3; § 349-f, as amd. by L. 1955, ch. 809) and under the related chapters 806 to 808 of the Laws of 1955 and under the home rule amendment of the State Constitution (art. IX, § 11) and under the New York City Charter and under the decision in Whalen v. Wagner (4 N Y 2d 575), the New York City Board of Estimate may approve a proposed 7th Avenue Approach or Gowanus Expressway to the proposed Narrows Bridge connecting Brooklyn and Staten Island as part of an interstate bridge and highway system with New Jersey under the jurisdiction of the Port of New York Authority, and may appropriate moneys for said expressway and may exercise the power of eminent domain for said expressway. Taxpayers' action against the city officials (General Municipal Law, § 51) is dismissed.

 

2565 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


O'Connor v. Eppig


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, New York County


14 Misc. 2d 641; 181 N.Y.S.2d 456; 1958 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2423



October 31, 1958 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner, an applicant for a court clerk position, brought a proceeding under N.Y. Civ. Prac. Act § 78 to challenge an action made by respondents, the Department of Personnel and Civil Service Commission, an applicant, and others. Respondent applicant was appointed to the clerk position, and petitioner sought to have it vacated and to compel his own appointment.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner and respondent applicant (respondent) were placed on regular promotion eligible list based on the results of a competitive examination. Petitioner had the highest grade on this list when a vacancy occurred for a clerkship, and respondent was the only other interested applicant. Petitioner challenged a decision to give respondent the position. On appeal, the court first noted that petitioner was protected by N.Y. Mil. Law § 243(1)(b), and was entitled a position on the eligible lists. However, N.Y. Const. art. V, § 6, did not absolutely entitled him to an appointment, and no rights "vested" when a name was put on such a list. Rather, the right to absolute preference for veterans, which was provided under a 1948 version of the constitution, was constitutionally withdrawn by a later 1950 amendment. The 1950 version mandated only that petitioner was granted five points additional credit, which was done. Finally, where petitioner and respondent were the only remaining eligible and interested candidates, the appointing authority was permitted to make a choice between them.


OUTCOME: The court denied the application.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Civil service -- veterans -- assistant clerk of New York City Municipal Court was in military service from 1942 to 1955 and was promoted from ranks to lieutenant and eventually to major, and was honorably discharged as disabled, and was restored to his position, and was given promotion examination comparable to those which had been given in 1948 and 1953; on such examination he earned 76.25 points and was given 5 points more as disabled veteran and was thus certified as No. 1 on promotion list bearing 7 names; vacancy occurred in 1957, but all 7 persons on list declined appointment except said No. 1 and No. 6; appointment of No. 6 was valid -- constitutional amendment of 1945 giving disabled veterans absolute preference was validly amended in 1950 so as to provide only for additional points (N. Y. Const., art. V, § 6); Military Law ( § 243, subd. 5) did not give to veteran rights as to 1948 examination which were not changed by constitutional amendment of 1950 -- on special list made in 1956 as of 1948, certification of this veteran for "absolute preference" was void -- since special list as of 1953, bore only this one name, it was within descretion of appointing authority to  [***2] disregard it -- appointment of No. 6 on regular list, though dated three days before date of certification of said list, was not actually premature but was valid -- veteran did not lose his civil service rights by remaining in military service between 1947 and 1950 (Military Law, § 243, subd. 1, par. [b]), or by not applying for discharge, or by accepting promotions in military service; he was rightly given additional points and put No. 1 on general promotion list, but was not legally entitled to promotion as against No. 6; his application for order (Civ. Prac. Act, art. 78) is denied.

1. The New York State Constitution (art. V, § 6) provides that appointments and promotions in civil service shall be made pursuant to competitive examinations as far as practicable. This was amended in 1945 (eff. Jan. 1, 1946) to provide, however, that disabled, honorably discharged veterans "shall be appointed or promoted before any other appointments or promotions are made, without regard to his or her standing on" the eligible list. This proviso was changed on January 1, 1950 to provide that, after January 1, 1951, honorably discharged veterans should receive certain additional points on [***3] examinations, and that eligible lists in existence on January 1, 1951 should be revised by adding such points to veterans' grades on such lists. The Civil Service Law ( § 14) provides that appointments shall be made "from among those graded highest in * * * examinations"; and this provision has been validly concretized by the Rules of the New York City Civil Service Commission (rule V, §§ VII, VIII) to provide that an appointment shall be made from among the three eligibles who are highest after any prior eligibles on the list have declined appointment. An assistant court clerk in the New York City Municipal Court, who was also a member of the New York National Guard, was in active military service from October 1940 to October 1941, and then from 1942 to 1955 during which time he was promoted to Major. He was honorably discharged as a disabled veteran on August 31, 1955, and was restored the next day to his position as a deputy clerk. Meantime, two promotion examinations for positions of clerks of district courts of the New York City Municipal Court had been held and eligible lists established in 1948 and 1953. This returned veteran was given a comparable examination (Military [***4] Law, § 243, subd. 5), on which he earned 76.25 points. With 5 additional points by reason of being a disabled veteran (N. Y. Const., art. V, § 6, as amd. eff. Jan. 1, 1950) he was thus given 81.25 points, and with this mark he was placed highest on the 1953 list. In 1957 a vacancy occurred in the position of clerk of one of the Manhattan district courts of the Municipal Court. All of the 7 persons on this 1953 eligible list declined appointment, except this veteran and the person who stood sixth on the list. The appointing Justices of the Municipal Court validly appointed eligible No. 6 to the position.

2. The constitutional amendment in 1950 (N. Y. Const., art. V, § 6) validly and constitutionally revoked the absolute preference which had been given to disabled veterans by the constitutional amendment of said section in 1945, and instead thereof it gave to veterans certain additional points on examinations, and provided that eligible lists in existence on January 1, 1951 should be revised by adding such points to the grades of veterans on such lists. Thus, the City Civil Service Commission no longer had power to grade this returned veteran as having an "absolute preference"  [***5] for appointment on a special promotion list bearing this veteran's name only, which it established in 1956 as of 1948 as a result of his attaining a mark of 76.25 on an examination comparable to that given in 1948 (Military Law, § 243, subd. 5). The Military Law provision gave him no vested rights as against a subsequent constitutional amendment. His grade of "absolute preference" on this list is void.

3. Before making this promotion, the appointing Justices of the Municipal Court canvassed all the eligibles on the list and personally interviewed them. The budgetary requisition was dated February 15, 1957, and the 1953 list was certified by the City Civil Service Commission to the Justices on February 18, and the Justices' appointment of eligible No. 6 was dated February 15. Said civil service commission has specified that promotions may be dated as of the date of the budget certification. Moreover, the "notice of appointment and disposition of certification" was dated March 1, 1957. The appointment was not made prematurely.

4. As a result of the comparable examination, the City Civil Service Commission also established a special promotion list as of 1953, on which this veteran [***6] was the sole eligible, and on which the commission gave him a grade of 76.25+5=81.25. But, since this list had less than 3 names, the appointing authority had the power to disregard it and to use the regular 1953 list, which bore 7 names.

5. Although the Military Law ( § 243, subd. 5) mentions "special" lists, the Legislature did not intend to deny to veterans the privileges of regular lists.

6. Although only 2 names remained on this regular eligible list after 5 eligibles declined, the appointing authority still had the power to make an appointment from this list.

7. This returned veteran was restored to his position of deputy clerk on September 1, 1955, and he filed an application on October 31, 1955 to take a "comparable" promotion examination (Military Law, § 243, subd. 5). This was the 60th day and was timely (General Construction Law, § 20). His request was granted by the official authorities having jurisdiction, and such grant is presumed to be regular.

8. This veteran's status as an honorably discharged, disabled veteran was shown by his official certification (Military Law, § 243, subd. 17), and no evidence to the contrary was offered.

9. In this proceeding by this [***7] veteran, under article 78 of the Civil Practice Act, the answer of eligible No. 6 alleges that he, too, is a disabled veteran. However, it does not appear that he requested the City Civil Service Commission to change his standing on the list in any way. He was not certified as an eligible veteran.

10. The Military Law ( § 243, subd. 1, par. [b]) excepts from its privileges military service "entered upon voluntarily" on or after January 1, 1947 and before June 25, 1950. This veteran was in military service from 1940 to 1941, and then again from 1942 to 1955. He was a sergeant from January 1942 to August 1942, when he became a lieutenant in the United States Army Reserve, on active duty. World War II began in 1941 and, according to Presidential proclamations, did not officially terminate until 1952, and by that time the Korean conflict of 1950 was in progress, and during all that time a reserve officer had no right to a discharge (U. S. Code, tit. 10, § 3492; 66 U. S. Stat. 330). His appointment as a captain on October 9, 1945 extended his term as an officer until October 9, 1950. He could have asked for a discharge from military service; but the above-quoted exception in [***8] the Military Law is as to service "entered upon" during certain dates, not as to service continued in during those dates. His remaining in the military service for many years, and his promotions from the ranks to lieutenant and then to captain and then to major, did not subject him to the penalty of losing his civil service rights. Those rights were reaffirmed by the appointing Justices of the Municipal Court (N. Y. City Mun. Ct. Code, § 7-a, subd. 1) by their reinstatement of him in 1955 in his former position of deputy clerk.

11. It is alleged that, after this veteran was reinstated, he declined an offer of promotion to the position of clerk of one of the Bronx district courts of the Municipal Court. Such declination did not affect his right to apply subsequently for appointment to such a clerkship in a Manhattan district of the court; indeed, such offer shows that the City Civil Service Commission and the appointing Justices recognized that he had been validly reinstated and was validly entitled to apply for promotion. They themselves decided the question of fact -- whether this veteran had impliedly resigned from civil service -- in his favor.

12. This veteran's institution [***9] of this review proceeding under article 78 of the Civil Practice Act was the proper remedy; but he has not shown that he is entitled to the promotion as a matter of law or that eligible No. 6 was not legally promoted; hence this veteran's application is denied.

 

2566 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Ten Fifth Avenue Corp. v. Baker


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Municipal Court of the City of New York, Borough of Manhattan


14 Misc. 2d 775; 179 N.Y.S.2d 288; 1958 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2442



October 30, 1958 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The court grants a final order to the tenant dismissing the petition.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff landlord sought judgment on its action to dispossess defendant tenant pursuant to N.Y. Bus. Rent Law § 8(gg)(1). The landlord contended that the tenant had neglected and refused to accept and execute a new lease for the store premises.


OVERVIEW: A landlord instituted an eviction action against the tenant seeking to dispossess the same pursuant to the Business Rent Law, 1956 N.Y. Laws 735, § 2. The tenant originally signed a five-year lease and remained in possession of the premises as a statutory tenant after the expiration of that lease for another seven years. The landlord contended that the tenant refused to accept and execute a new lease, that six months had elapsed subsequent to the offer of such lease, and that the landlord had given notice to the tenant to vacate the premises. The court granted a final order to the tenant dismissing the landlord's eviction action on grounds that the landlord's proffered lease was not substantially similar in terms and conditions as the existing lease or tenancy. The court held that the landlord was required to comply with the Business Rent Law by offering the tenant a lease on substantially the same terms and conditions, and the tenant was not obligated to state his reasons for rejecting the lease until the landlord complied with such statute. The new lease imposed a greater obligation on the tenant in that it should comply with all laws and orders at its sole expense.


OUTCOME: The court granted a final order to the tenant dismissing the landlord's petition to dispossess the tenant of certain store premises.


HEADNOTES

Landlord and tenant -- rent regulation -- removal of tenant -- tenant may not be dispossessed from store property under Business Rent Law for rejecting proffered lease when its terms and conditions are not substantially similar to those of existing tenancy, although other ground was stated for rejection -- landlord must show substantial similarity of terms -- landlord not entitled to evict as terms of new lease were shown to be substantially different (Business Rent Law, § 8, subd. gg).

1. Tenant in possession of a store as a statutory tenant since the expiration in 1949 of a five-year lease was sent a registered letter by the landlord on December 9, 1957, containing an offer to enter into a proffered lease for a term to commence on January 1, 1958 and to expire on June 30, 1958. The tenant refused to enter into such a lease, giving as his reason the desire for a long-term lease. After the expiration of six months, the landlord served a statutory 30-day notice to dispossess the tenant pursuant to subdivision gg of section 8 of the Business Rent Law, which authorizes eviction under these circumstances [***2] if the proffered (and rejected) lease was on substantially similar terms and conditions as the existing tenancy. As the tenant was under no obligation to state his grounds for rejecting the proffered lease and as he cannot waive the provisions of the Business Rent Law, the ground given by him for rejecting the lease does not limit him to that ground, and the landlord is not entitled to evict if, in fact, the terms of the proffered lease were not substantially similar to those of the existing tenancy.

2. The landlord must show as part of his prima facie case that the proffered lease was substantially similar to the former lease.

3. As the new lease contained a number of terms and conditions which enlarged upon the obligations of the tenant and put a greater onus upon him than did the terms of the prior lease, substantial differences do exist. Accordingly, the petition to dispossess the tenant is dismissed.

 

2567 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Zehil v. Weaver


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Kings County


15 Misc. 2d 436; 182 N.Y.S.2d 111; 1958 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2499



October 20, 1958 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Since the determination has support in the record and may not be said to be arbitrary or capricious, the petition is dismissed.

Settle order on notice.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner landlord sought review of a determination by respondent State Rent Administrator (New York) denying his certificate of eviction, finding that his application failed to establish an immediate and compelling necessity as required by State Rent and Eviction Regulations § 55. The Administrator also denied the landlord's protest, which alleged that janitorial services could not be maintained because property expenses exceeded the income.


OVERVIEW: The landlord, his wife, and their son-in-law purchased a six-family dwelling in April 1957. The son-in-law and his wife moved into a vacant apartment in the building. The landlord, his wife, and their two sons live elsewhere in a five-room apartment. The landlord filed an application for his tenant's four-room apartment, asserting that his daughter was pregnant, that the mother wanted to be near the daughter to take care of her, that the premises did not contain a janitor, and that the son-in-law and daughter both worked and could not properly care for the property. The Administrator denied the landlord's application for failure to establish an immediate and compelling necessity. On appeal, the court held that the Administrator's determination was supported by the record and was not arbitrary or capricious. The court rejected the argument that § 55 of the State Rent and Eviction Regulations was unconstitutional by distinguishing multiple family housing from one or two-family homes. The court held that the distinction it drew between commercial property and single-family or two-family houses was reasonable for purposes of Equal Protection Clause analysis.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed the landlord's petition for judicial review of the Administrator's denial of his application for a certificate of eviction and the denial of his protest.


HEADNOTES

Landlord and tenant -- rent regulation -- immediate and compelling necessity -- application to remove tenant from four-room apartment on ground that petitioner's daughter who lived in building was pregnant, that her mother wanted to take care of daughter, that premises did not contain janitor and that daughter and son-in-law could not care for property denied for failure to establish immediate and compelling necessity -- determination supported by record and not arbitrary or capricious -- section 55 of State Rent and Eviction Regulations which requires finding of immediate necessity before certificate issued not unconstitutional.

1. Petitioner, his wife and their son-in-law purchased a six-family dwelling in April, 1957 and the son-in-law and his wife moved into a vacant apartment therein. Petitioner filed an application for a tenant's four-room apartment on the ground that his daughter was pregnant, that her mother wanted to be near her daughter to take care of her, that the premises did not contain a janitor and that [***2] the son-in-law and daughter both worked and could not properly care for the property. The application was denied for failure of petitioner to establish an immediate and compelling necessity. The determination is supported by the record and is not arbitrary or capricious and the proceeding to review the determination is dismissed.

2. Section 55 of the State Rent and Eviction Regulations which requires a finding of an immediate and compelling necessity before a certificate may be issued, is not unconstitutional. The distinction made between commercial property and one- or two-family houses is reasonable.

 

2568 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Hammond


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


City Court of New York, Corning


14 Misc. 2d 607; 183 N.Y.S.2d 426; 1958 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2503



October 20, 1958 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] In view of the foregoing, the court holds that the particular parts of this section of the law upon which this arrest was made are invalid because unconstitutional, and the charge against the defendant is therefore dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant filed a motion to dismiss charges against him for leaving his car standing unattended upon a public street without first turning off his engine, locking the ignition, and removing the keys, in violation of N.Y. Veh. & Traf. Law § 1210(a) (1957). Defendant challenged the statute as unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was arrested for leaving his car standing unattended upon a public street without first turning off his engine, locking the ignition, and removing the keys, in violation of N.Y. Veh. & Traf. Law § 1210(a) (1957). He filed a motion to dismiss the charges against him on the ground that § 1210(a) was unconstitutional. The court dismissed the charges against defendant because the law in § 1210(a) was invalid and unconstitutional. The court held that the guarantees of personal freedom under the Constitution afforded protection from laws such as § 1210(a). Simply put, the statute was unconstitutional because it subjected defendant to criminal penalties for violating a traffic regulation designed to protect him from the possible unlawful act of another.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed the charges against defendant because the law upon which the arrest was made was invalid as it was unconstitutional.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- unattended automobile -- Vehicle and Traffic Law ( § 1210, subd. [a]), providing that motor vehicle shall not be permitted to stand unattended without stopping engine, locking ignition and removing key, unconstitutional.

Defendant was arrested upon a charge of leaving his car standing unattended upon a public street without first turning off his engine, locking the ignition and removing the keys, in violation of the Vehicle and Traffic Law ( § 1210, subd. [a]) which provides that: "No person driving or in charge of a motor vehicle shall permit it to stand unattended without first stopping the engine, locking the ignition, removing the key, and effectively setting the brake thereon and, when standing upon any grade, turning the front wheels to the curb or side of the highway." The arrest was made upon that part of the statute which relates to the ignition and the key. Those parts of the statute are invalid because unconstitutional. The [***2] charge against defendant is therefore dismissed. The guarantees of personal freedom under the Constitution protect against this type of governmental regulation of daily lives.

 

2569 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


McManus v. De Sapio


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Trial Term, Albany County


13 Misc. 2d 513; 182 N.Y.S.2d 516; 1958 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2515



October 17, 1958 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner voters sought review of the determination of defendant, Secretary of State (secretary), which rejected the voters' independent nominating petitions designating certain individuals as candidates for nomination for public office to be voted for at the general election.


OVERVIEW: The secretary rejected numerous signatures on the voters' petitions for various reasons including that the voter signed an abbreviated version of his name and that the subscribing witness witnesses' notarized statement had been altered. The court reversed the secretary's determination. Although the court concluded that in some instances the secretary had been correct in invalidating certain signatures, when the total number of invalidated signatures was calculated, the petitions contained enough valid signatures to be deemed legally acceptable. The court concluded that an abbreviated signature was permissible under N.Y. Elec. Law § 138-a. The court determined that the change of address shown by deleting the typed address and inserting a writing address and the deletion of a numeral designation for the month followed by the insertion of the month spelled out alphabetically were not material alterations and were not fatal to the validity of the petitions. The court opined, however, that the change in the number of the signatures to which the subscribing witness certified was a material alteration.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decision of the secretary and concluded that the voters' petitions designating certain individuals as candidates for nomination for public office were entitled to be and should have been filed in accordance with the law.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Elections -- independent nominating petitions -- signers of independent nominating petitions may abbreviate their given names ( Election Law, § 138-a), and may use "Scho" for "Schoharie" County, and may give incomplete residence addresses in election districts where no personal registration is required -- "Statement of Witness" to signatures ( Election Law, § 138, subd. 3) may, on repeating his own address, state "same" and then strike out "same" and repeat address; also may state "55th election district" for district recently changed to 52d -- but "Statement of Witness" cannot substitute "10" signatures for "6" signatures, or "5" for "6" -- requirement of 50 valid signatures for each county fulfilled; petition is valid ( Election Law, § 138, subd. 5, par. [a]).

1. Petitions for independent State-wide nominations require the signatures of at least 12,000 voters, of whom at least 50 must reside in each county of the State ( Election Law, § 138, subd. 5, par. [a]). The signatures must be acknowledged before a notary, or must be authenticated by a "Statement of Witness" in the form set forth in the Election Law ( § 138, subd. 3) with the blanks in such witness' statement [***2] properly filled in. Such a "Statement", reading that the witness resides at 98 Attorney St., in the City and County of New York in New York State, and "was last registered for the general election in the year 1957 from same [a line is drawn through the word "same"] 98 Attorney St. in the City of N. Y. in the county of New York in such state" is valid. The striking out of "same" and substituting therefor its obvious meaning "98 Attorney St." was not a material alteration, and no fraud was proved. Similarly, in another witness' "Statement", an alteration consisting of striking "N. Y." and substituting therefor "New York" is not material, and this "Statement", too, is valid.

2. Another witness resided at all times in the 4th Assembly District in New York City at one address, but in 1956 that address was changed from the 55th Election District in said Assembly District to the 52d Election District in said Assembly District, effective about October 1, 1957. This witness' "Statement of Witness" erroneously gave her election district as the 55th; but this error was not prejudicial, and her "Statement" is valid.

3. However, another "Statement of Witness", in which the number of signatures [***3] thereby authenticated was stated to be "6" and in which this figure was then struck out and the figure "10" substituted, is materially altered and entirely invalid.

4. Similarly entirely invalid is another "Statement of Witness" to authenticate "6" signatures, where "6" was struck out and "5" was inserted.

5. A signature on an independent nominating petition may be abbreviated ( Election Law, § 138-a). Accordingly, on this independent nominating petition, the signature "Bert Funk" for the registered name Bertram Funk is valid, as is the signature "G. R. Smith" for the registered name Gordon G. R. Smith, as is the signature "Rose Amlin" for Rosetta Amlin, and as is the signature "Ted Pierce" for Theodore Pierce.

6. Signatures where the county of the address is written as "Schoharie" above the abbreviation "Scho" are valid.

7. Subdivision 6 of section 138 of the Election Law, providing that "The name of a person signing such a petition for an election for which voters are required to be registered shall not be counted if such person was not registered at the time of the last preceding general election as a qualified voter", is constitutional and valid.

8. In rural election districts,  [***4] personal registration is not required, and the voter is not required to appear prior to election day ( Election Law, §§ 157, 166-a). In such districts, the name of the voter is copied from year to year by the inspectors of election, because the voter voted at the previous election or because the inspectors know that he still resides within that election district. The books of registration in districts in Schoharie County in this case showed that in many instances no address was inserted and in some instances ditto marks were used to designate the address; and it is apparent that the voter himself did not furnish the address as reported by the inspectors of election. The only time when a voter in such a district is required to sign his name is at the time of voting, and the only use of such signature is for a primary election ( Election Law, § 198). Accordingly, on this independent nominating petition, signatures with incomplete addresses are valid, where such addresses are addresses used by persons residing within such election district and where the signers are found to have been registered and where there is nothing to show that these signers had any knowledge of the addresses [***5] contained in the registration book, or where the registration book contains no addresses for these names.

9. Accordingly, the Secretary of State erred in rejecting this independent nominating petition, and he is directed to accept it.

 

2570 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Newcomb v. Talento


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Westchester County


16 Misc. 2d 148; 188 N.Y.S.2d 568; 1958 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2487



October 11, 1958 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] October 21, 1958.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner signers of a certain petition filed an application to compel submission to the electorate of a local law for the creation of a commission to draft a new charter. Respondent city clerk opposed the submission based on a rule that the petition could not be submitted in an even-numbered year and that the proposed local law was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: The proposed local law was to permit political city committees to appoint 9 out of 11 members of the charter commission. The court held that N.Y. Const. art. IX, § 15 regarding the election of city officers in odd-numbered years was clear and that only by extending its meaning could referenda in even-numbered years be prohibited. The court found that the appointing body would be as observant of the requirements of N.Y. Pub. Off. Law as would the mayor or city council. The court dismissed the petition, ruling that unless it was shown that a city committee was actually provided for by party rules and that there was some regulation over its management, the power of appointment of the commission members was subject to no regulation at all. The court noted that an enactment of legislation that made for such a situation was not within the framework of the New York Constitution. On appeal, the court concluded that the signers had supplied the allegation and proof of the existence of city committees. The court indicated that the objections urged as to the validity of the local law could be raised when and if the law were adopted.


OUTCOME: The court granted the signers' petition to compel submission to the electorate of the local law.


HEADNOTES

Elections -- referenda -- referendum of municipal question involving creation of commission to draft new charter may be had in even-numbered year -- proposed law permitting appointment of some members of proposed charter commission by city committees not invalid when provision for such committees and their management by party rules is shown -- objections regarding validity of proposed law can be raised when and if law is adopted.

1. A local law for creation of a commission to draft a new charter was initiated by petition under subdivision 2 of section 20 of the City Home Rule Law. Section 15 of article IX of the New York Constitution requires election of all city officers in odd-numbered years. It is no objection that this referendum is sought in an even-numbered year. See Salducco v. Etkin (268 N. Y. 606) and City of Buffalo v. Lawley (6 A D 2d 66).

2. The proposed law would permit city committees to appoint some of the members of the proposed commission. Since section 10 of the Election Law affords no status to political city committees in the absence of rules of the party and since section 15 of the Election Law deprives such committees [***2] of power to adopt their own rules for management, the proposed law would be invalid as not within the framework of the constitution unless the city committees are actually provided for by party rules and there is some regulation over their management. However, as petitioners have supplied the allegations and proof of the existence of city committees, this petition to compel submission to the electorate will be granted.

3. Objections as to the validity of the local law can be raised when and if the law is adopted.
